<meta name="DC.Description" content="Fixed nitrogen is essential for plant growth. Some plants, such as legumes, can host nitrogen-fixing bacteria within cells in root organs called nodules. Nodules are considered to have evolved in parallel in different lineages, but the genetic changes underlying this evolution remain unknown. Based on gene expression in the nitrogen-fixing nonlegume Parasponia andersonii and the legume Medicago truncatula , we find that nodules in these different lineages may share a single origin. Comparison of the genomes of Parasponia with those of related nonnodulating plants reveals evidence of parallel loss of genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation. Taken together, this raises the possibility that nodulation originated only once and was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages." />

<meta name="article:section" content="PNAS Plus" />
<meta name="citation_title" content="Comparative genomics of the nonlegume Parasponia reveals insights into evolution of nitrogen-fixing rhizobium symbioses" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Nodules harboring nitrogen-fixing rhizobia are a well-known trait of legumes, but nodules also occur in other plant lineages, with rhizobia or the actinomycete &lt;i&gt;Frankia&lt;/i&gt; as microsymbiont. It is generally assumed that nodulation evolved independently multiple times. However, molecular-genetic support for this hypothesis is lacking, as the genetic changes underlying nodule evolution remain elusive. We conducted genetic and comparative genomics studies by using &lt;i&gt;Parasponia&lt;/i&gt; species (Cannabaceae), the only nonlegumes that can establish nitrogen-fixing nodules with rhizobium. Intergeneric crosses between &lt;i&gt;Parasponia andersonii&lt;/i&gt; and its nonnodulating relative &lt;i&gt;Trema tomentosa&lt;/i&gt; demonstrated that nodule organogenesis, but not intracellular infection, is a dominant genetic trait. Comparative transcriptomics of &lt;i&gt;P. andersonii&lt;/i&gt; and the legume &lt;i&gt;Medicago truncatula&lt;/i&gt; revealed utilization of at least 290 orthologous symbiosis genes in nodules. Among these are key genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation, including &lt;i&gt;NODULE INCEPTION&lt;/i&gt; (&lt;i&gt;NIN&lt;/i&gt;) and &lt;i&gt;RHIZOBIUM-DIRECTED POLAR GROWTH&lt;/i&gt; (&lt;i&gt;RPG&lt;/i&gt;). Comparative analysis of genomes from three &lt;i&gt;Parasponia&lt;/i&gt; species and related nonnodulating plant species show evidence of parallel loss in nonnodulating species of putative orthologs of &lt;i&gt;NIN&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;RPG&lt;/i&gt;, and &lt;i&gt;NOD FACTOR PERCEPTION&lt;/i&gt;. Parallel loss of these symbiosis genes indicates that these nonnodulating lineages lost the potential to nodulate. Taken together, our results challenge the view that nodulation evolved in parallel and raises the possibility that nodulation originated ∼100 Mya in a common ancestor of all nodulating plant species, but was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages. This will have profound implications for translational approaches aimed at engineering nitrogen-fixing nodules in crop plants.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Fixed nitrogen is essential for plant growth. Some plants, such as legumes, can host nitrogen-fixing bacteria within cells in root organs called nodules. Nodules are considered to have evolved in parallel in different lineages, but the genetic changes underlying this evolution remain unknown. Based on gene expression in the nitrogen-fixing nonlegume &lt;i&gt;Parasponia andersonii&lt;/i&gt; and the legume &lt;i&gt;Medicago truncatula&lt;/i&gt;, we find that nodules in these different lineages may share a single origin. Comparison of the genomes of &lt;i&gt;Parasponia&lt;/i&gt; with those of related nonnodulating plants reveals evidence of parallel loss of genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation. Taken together, this raises the possibility that nodulation originated only once and was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="citation_reference" content="citation_journal_title=Bioinformatics;citation_author=Y. Liao;citation_author=GK. Smyth;citation_author=W. <meta name="twitter:title" content="Comparative genomics of the nonlegume Parasponia reveals insights into evolution of nitrogen-fixing rhizobium symbioses" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/115/20/E4700/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Fixed nitrogen is essential for plant growth. Some plants, such as legumes, can host nitrogen-fixing bacteria within cells in root organs called nodules. Nodules are considered to have evolved in parallel in different lineages, but the genetic changes underlying this evolution remain unknown. Based on gene expression in the nitrogen-fixing nonlegume Parasponia andersonii and the legume Medicago truncatula , we find that nodules in these different lineages may share a single origin. Comparison of the genomes of Parasponia with those of related nonnodulating plants reveals evidence of parallel loss of genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation. Taken together, this raises the possibility that nodulation originated only once and was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Fixed nitrogen is essential for plant growth. Some plants, such as legumes, can host nitrogen-fixing bacteria within cells in root organs called nodules. Nodules are considered to have evolved in parallel in different lineages, but the genetic changes underlying this evolution remain unknown. Based on gene expression in the nitrogen-fixing nonlegume Parasponia andersonii and the legume Medicago truncatula , we find that nodules in these different lineages may share a single origin. Comparison of the genomes of Parasponia with those of related nonnodulating plants reveals evidence of parallel loss of genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation. Taken together, this raises the possibility that nodulation originated only once and was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="809511" id="node809511" data-pisa="pnas;115/20/E4700" data-pisa-master="pnas;1721395115" data-apath="/pnas/115/20/E4700.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">

      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Comparative genomics of the nonlegume <em>Parasponia</em> reveals insights into evolution of nitrogen-fixing rhizobium symbioses</h1>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-6">Fixed nitrogen is essential for plant growth. Some plants, such as legumes, can host nitrogen-fixing bacteria within cells in root organs called nodules. Nodules are considered to have evolved in parallel in different lineages, but the genetic changes underlying this evolution remain unknown. Based on gene expression in the nitrogen-fixing nonlegume <em>Parasponia andersonii</em> and the legume <em>Medicago truncatula</em>, we find that nodules in these different lineages may share a single origin. Comparison of the genomes of <em>Parasponia</em> with those of related nonnodulating plants reveals evidence of parallel loss of genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation. Taken together, this raises the possibility that nodulation originated only once and was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">Nodules harboring nitrogen-fixing rhizobia are a well-known trait of legumes, but nodules also occur in other plant lineages, with rhizobia or the actinomycete <em>Frankia</em> as microsymbiont. It is generally assumed that nodulation evolved independently multiple times. However, molecular-genetic support for this hypothesis is lacking, as the genetic changes underlying nodule evolution remain elusive. We conducted genetic and comparative genomics studies by using <em>Parasponia</em> species (Cannabaceae), the only nonlegumes that can establish nitrogen-fixing nodules with rhizobium. Intergeneric crosses between <em>Parasponia andersonii</em> and its nonnodulating relative <em>Trema tomentosa</em> demonstrated that nodule organogenesis, but not intracellular infection, is a dominant genetic trait. Comparative transcriptomics of <em>P. andersonii</em> and the legume <em>Medicago truncatula</em> revealed utilization of at least 290 orthologous symbiosis genes in nodules. Among these are key genes that, in legumes, are essential for nodulation, including <em>NODULE INCEPTION</em> (<em>NIN</em>) and <em>RHIZOBIUM-DIRECTED POLAR GROWTH</em> (<em>RPG</em>). Comparative analysis of genomes from three <em>Parasponia</em> species and related nonnodulating plant species show evidence of parallel loss in nonnodulating species of putative orthologs of <em>NIN</em>, <em>RPG</em>, and <em>NOD FACTOR PERCEPTION</em>. Parallel loss of these symbiosis genes indicates that these nonnodulating lineages lost the potential to nodulate. Taken together, our results challenge the view that nodulation evolved in parallel and raises the possibility that nodulation originated ∼100 Mya in a common ancestor of all nodulating plant species, but was subsequently lost in many descendant lineages. This will have profound implications for translational approaches aimed at engineering nitrogen-fixing nodules in crop plants.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/symbiosis" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-symbiosis" rel="nofollow">symbiosis</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/biological-nitrogen-fixation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-biological-nitrogen-fixation" rel="nofollow">biological nitrogen fixation</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-evolution" rel="nofollow">evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/comparative-genomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-comparative-genomics" rel="nofollow">comparative genomics</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/copy-number-variation-0" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-copy-number-variation" rel="nofollow">copy number variation</a></li></ul><p id="p-8" class="flushleft">Nitrogen sources such as nitrate or ammonia are key nutrients for plant growth, but their availability is frequently limited. Some plant species in the related orders Fabales, Fagales, Rosales, and Cucurbitales—collectively known as the nitrogen-fixing clade—can overcome this limitation by establishing a nitrogen-fixing endosymbiosis with <em>Frankia</em> or rhizobium bacteria (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). These symbioses require specialized root organs, known as nodules, that provide optimal physiological conditions for nitrogen fixation (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). For example, nodules of legumes (Fabaceae, order Fabales) contain a high concentration of hemoglobin that is essential to control oxygen homeostasis and protect the rhizobial nitrogenase enzyme complex from oxidation (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Legumes, such as soybean (<em>Glycine max</em>), common bean (<em>Phaseolus vulgaris</em>), and peanut (<em>Arachis hypogaea</em>), represent the only crops that possess nitrogen-fixing nodules, and engineering this trait in other crop plants is a long-term vision in sustainable agriculture (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>).</p><p id="p-9">Nodulating plants represent ∼10 related clades that diverged &gt;100 Mya, supporting a shared evolutionary origin of the underlying capacity for this trait (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Nevertheless, these nodulating clades are interspersed with many nonnodulating lineages. This has led to two hypotheses explaining the evolution of nodulation (<a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). The first is that nodulation has a single origin in the root of the nitrogen-fixation clade, followed by multiple independent losses. The second is that nodulation originated independently multiple times, preceded by a single hypothetical predisposition event in a common ancestor of the nitrogen-fixing fixation clade. The latter of these hypotheses is more widely accepted (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a><a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-7"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-9"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-10"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-11"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>).</p><p id="p-10">Genetic dissection of rhizobium symbiosis in two legume models—<em>Medicago truncatula</em> (medicago) and <em>Lotus japonicus</em> (lotus)—has uncovered symbiosis genes that are essential for nodule organogenesis, bacterial infection, and nitrogen fixation (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). These include genes encoding LysM-type receptors that perceive rhizobial lipochitooligosaccharides (LCOs; also known as Nod factors) and transcriptionally activate the <em>NODULE INCEPTION</em> (<em>NIN</em>) transcription factor (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a><a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-14"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-15"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-16"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-17"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). Expression of <em>NIN</em> is essential and sufficient to set in motion nodule organogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a><a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-20"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>). Some symbiosis genes have been coopted from the more ancient and widespread arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>, <a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>). However, causal genetic differences between nodulating and nonnodulating species have not been identified (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>).</p><p id="p-11">To obtain insight into the molecular-genetic changes underlying evolution of nitrogen-fixing root nodules, we conducted comparative studies by using <em>Parasponia</em> (Cannabaceae, order Rosales). The genus <em>Parasponia</em> is the only lineage outside the legume family establishing a nodule symbiosis with rhizobium (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a><a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-26"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-27"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Similarly as shown for legumes, nodule formation in <em>Parasponia</em> is initiated by rhizobium-secreted LCOs (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a><a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-30"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). This suggests that <em>Parasponia</em> and legumes use a similar set of genes to control nodulation, but the extent of common gene use between distantly related nodulating species remains unknown. The genus <em>Parasponia</em> represents a clade of five species that is phylogenetically embedded in the closely related <em>Trema</em> genus (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). Like <em>Parasponia</em> and most other land plants, <em>Trema</em> species can establish an arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>). However, they are nonresponsive to rhizobium LCOs and do not form nodules (<a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>, <a id="xref-ref-31-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). Taken together, <em>Parasponia</em> is an excellent system for comparative studies with legumes and nonnodulating <em>Trema</em> species to provide insights into the molecular-genetic changes underlying evolution of nitrogen-fixing root nodules.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Nodule Organogenesis Is a Genetically Dominant Trait.</h3><p id="p-12">First, we took a genetics approach to understanding the rhizobium symbiosis trait of <em>Parasponia</em> by making intergeneric crosses (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>). Viable F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants were obtained only from the cross <em>Parasponia andersonii</em> (2n = 20) × <em>Trema tomentosa</em> (2n = 4x = 40; <a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2</a>). These triploid hybrids (2n = 3x = 30) were infertile, but could be propagated clonally. We noted that F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants formed root nodules when grown in potting soil, similar to earlier observations for <em>P. andersonii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). To further investigate the nodulation phenotype of these hybrid plants, clonally propagated plants were inoculated with two different strains, <em>Bradyrhizobium elkanii</em> strain WUR3 (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>) or <em>Mesorhizobium plurifarium</em> strain BOR2. The latter strain was isolated from the rhizosphere of <em>Trema orientalis</em> in Malaysian Borneo and showed to be an effective nodulator of <em>P. andersonii</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). Both strains induced nodules on F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>B</em>, <em>D</em>, and <em>E</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>) but, as expected, not on <em>T. tomentosa</em>, nor on any other <em>Trema</em> species investigated. By using an acetylene reduction assay, we noted that, in contrast to <em>P. andersonii</em> nodules, in F<sub>1</sub> hybrid nodules of plant H9 infected with <em>M. plurifarium</em> BOR2 there is no nitrogenase activity (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>C</em></a>). To further examine this discrepancy, we studied the cytoarchitecture of these nodules. In <em>P. andersonii</em> nodules, apoplastic <em>M. plurifarium</em> BOR2 colonies infect cells to form so-called fixation threads (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>F</em> and <em>H</em>–<em>J</em></a>), whereas, in F<sub>1</sub> hybrid nodules, these colonies remain apoplastic and fail to establish intracellular infections (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>G</em> and <em>K</em></a>). To exclude the possibility that the lack of intracellular infection is caused by heterozygosity of <em>P. andersonii</em> whereby only a nonfunctional allele was transmitted to the F<sub>1</sub> hybrid genotype, or by the particular rhizobium strain used for this experiment, we examined five independent F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants inoculated with <em>M. plurifarium</em> BOR2 or <em>B. elkanii</em> WUR3. This revealed a lack of intracellular infection structures in nodules of all F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants tested, irrespective which of the two rhizobium strains was used (<a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>G</em> and <em>K</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>), confirming that heterozygosity of <em>P. andersonii</em> does not play a role in the F<sub>1</sub> hybrid infection phenotype. These results suggest, at least partly, independent genetic control of nodule organogenesis and rhizobium infection. Because F<sub>1</sub> hybrids are nodulated with similar efficiency as <em>P. andersonii</em> (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>), we conclude that the network controlling nodule organogenesis is genetically dominant.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Nodulation phenotype of P. andersonii and interspecific P. andersonii &#xD7; T. tomentosa F1 hybrid plants. CW, cell wall.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3><em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> Genomes Are Highly Similar.</h3><p id="p-14">Based on preliminary genome size estimates made by using FACS measurements, three <em>Parasponia</em> and five <em>Trema</em> species were selected for comparative genome analysis (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). K-mer analysis of medium-coverage genome sequence data (∼30×) revealed that all genomes had low levels of heterozygosity, except those of <em>Trema levigata</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> accession RG16 (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>). Based on these k-mer data, we also generated more accurate estimates of genome sizes. Additionally, we used these data to assemble chloroplast genomes, based on which we obtained additional phylogenetic evidence that <em>T. levigata</em> is sister to <em>Parasponia</em> (<a id="xref-fig-1-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S6–S8</a>). Graph-based clustering of repetitive elements in the genomes (calibrated with the genome size estimates based on k-mers) revealed that all selected species contain approximately 300 Mb of nonrepetitive sequence and a variable repeat content that correlates with the estimated genome size that ranges from 375 to 625 Mb (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S9 and Table S3</a>). Notably, we found a <em>Parasponia</em>-specific expansion of ogre/tat LTR retrotransposons comprising 65–85 Mb (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S9<em>B</em></a>). We then generated annotated reference genomes by using high-coverage (∼125×) sequencing of <em>P. andersonii</em> accession WU1 (<a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>) and <em>T. orientalis</em> accession RG33 (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S4 and S5</a>). These species were selected based on their low heterozygosity levels in combination with relatively small genomes. <em>T. tomentosa</em> was not used for a high-quality genome assembly because it is an allotetraploid (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5 and Tables S2 and S3</a>).</p><p id="p-15">We generated orthogroups for <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> genes and six other Eurosid species, including arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>) and the legumes medicago and soybean. From both <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em>, ∼35,000 genes could be clustered into &gt;20,000 orthogroups (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>; note that there can be multiple orthologous gene pairs per orthogroup). Within these orthogroups, we identified 25,605 <em>P. andersonii</em>–<em>T. orientalis</em> orthologous gene pairs based on phylogenetic analysis as well as whole-genome alignments (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>). These orthologous gene pairs had a median percentage nucleotide identity of 97% for coding regions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S10 and S11</a>). This further supports the recent divergence of the two species and facilitates their genomic comparison.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Common Utilization of Symbiosis Genes in <em>Parasponia</em> and Medicago.</h3><p id="p-16">To assess commonalities in the utilization of symbiosis genes in <em>Parasponia</em> species and legumes, we employed two strategies. First, we performed phylogenetic analyses of close homologs of genes that were characterized to function in legume–rhizobium symbiosis. This revealed that <em>P. andersonii</em> contains putative orthologs of the vast majority of these legume symbiosis genes (96 of 126; <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">S3</a>). Second, we compared the sets of genes with enhanced expression in nodules of <em>P. andersonii</em> and medicago. RNA sequencing of <em>P. andersonii</em> nodules revealed 1,719 genes that are functionally annotated and have a significantly enhanced expression level (fold change &gt;2, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.05, DESeq2 Wald test) in any of three nodule developmental stages compared with uninoculated roots (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>). For medicago, we generated a comparable data set of 2,753 nodule-enhanced genes based on published RNA sequencing data (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). We then determined the overlap of these two gene sets based on orthogroup membership and found that 382 orthogroups comprise both <em>P. andersonii</em> and medicago nodule-enhanced genes. This number is significantly greater than is to be expected by chance (permutation test, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.00001; <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S13</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). Based on phylogenetic analysis of these orthogroups, we found that in 290 cases putative orthologs have been utilized in <em>P. andersonii</em> and medicago root nodules (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S5</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">S6</a>). Among these 290 commonly utilized genes are 26 putative orthologs of legume symbiosis genes, e.g., the LCO-responsive transcription factor <em>NIN</em> and its downstream target <em>NUCLEAR TRANSCRIPTION FACTOR-YA1</em> (<em>NFYA1</em>) that are essential for nodule organogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>, <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>) and <em>RHIZOBIUM DIRECTED POLAR GROWTH</em> (<em>RPG</em>) involved in intracellular infection (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>). Of these 26, five are known to function also in arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis (namely <em>VAPYRIN</em>, <em>SYMBIOTIC REMORIN</em>, the transcription factors <em>CYCLOPS</em> and <em>SAT1</em>, and a cysteine proteinase gene) (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a><a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-39"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-40"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-41"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-42"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-43"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-44"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). To further assess whether commonly utilized genes may be coopted from the ancient and widespread arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis, we determined which fraction is also induced upon mycorrhization in medicago based on published RNA sequencing data (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). This revealed that only 8% of the commonly utilized genes have such induction in both symbioses (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>).</p><p id="p-17">By exploiting the insight that nodule organogenesis and rhizobial infection can be genetically dissected using hybrid plants, we classified these commonly utilized genes into two categories based on their expression profiles in roots and nodules of both <em>P. andersonii</em> and F<sub>1</sub> hybrids (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>). The first category comprises 126 genes that are up-regulated in both <em>P. andersonii</em> and hybrid nodules and that we associate with nodule organogenesis. The second category comprises 164 genes that are up-regulated in only the <em>P. andersonii</em> nodule and that we therefore associate with infection and/or fixation (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). Based on these results, we conclude that <em>Parasponia</em> and medicago utilize orthologous genes that commit various functions in at least two different developmental stages of the root nodule.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Clustering of commonly utilized symbiosis genes based on expression profile in P. andersonii. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-18" class="first-child">Clustering of commonly utilized symbiosis genes based on expression profile in <em>P. andersonii</em>. Cluster 1 (pink) represents genes related to nodule organogenesis: these genes are up-regulated in <em>P. andersonii</em> and hybrid nodules. Cluster 2 (green) represents genes related to infection and fixation: these genes are highly up-regulated in <em>P. andersonii</em> nodules but do not respond in the hybrid nodule. <em>PanBHLH109</em>, <em>BASIC HELIX–LOOP–HELIX DOMAIN CONTAINING PROTEIN 109</em>; <em>PanMATE8</em>, <em>MULTI ANTIMICROBIAL EXTRUSION PROTEIN 8</em>; <em>PanNOOT1, NODULE ROOT 1</em>; <em>PanNPF3, NITRATE/PEPTIDE TRANSPORTER FAMILY 3</em>; <em>PanPCO1, PLANT CYSTEINE OXIDASE 1.</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Lineage-Specific Adaptation in <em>Parasponia</em> HEMOGLOBIN 1.</h3><p id="p-19">Notable exceptions to the pattern of common utilization in root nodules are the oxygen-binding hemoglobins. Earlier studies showed that <em>Parasponia</em> and legumes have recruited different hemoglobin genes (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). Whereas legumes use class II LEGHEMOGLOBIN to control oxygen homeostasis, <em>Parasponia</em> recruited the paralogous class I <em>HEMOGLOBIN 1 (HB1)</em> for this function (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>A</em> and <em>B</em></a>). Biochemical studies have revealed that <em>P. andersonii</em> PanHB1 has oxygen affinities and kinetics that are adapted to their symbiotic function, whereas this is not the case for <em>T. tomentosa</em> TtoHB1 (<a id="xref-ref-47-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). We therefore examined HB1 from <em>Parasponia</em> species, <em>Trema</em> species, and other nonsymbiotic Rosales species to see if these differences are caused by a gain of function in <em>Parasponia</em> or a loss of function in the nonsymbiotic species. Based on protein alignment, we identified <em>Parasponia</em>-specific adaptations in 7 amino acids (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>C</em> and <em>D</em></a>). Among these is Ile(101), for which it is speculated to be causal for a functional change in <em>P. andersonii</em> HB1 (<a id="xref-ref-48-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). Hemoglobin-controlled oxygen homeostasis is crucial to protect the rhizobial nitrogen-fixing enzyme complex Nitrogenase in legume rhizobium-infected nodule cells (<a id="xref-ref-2-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Therefore, <em>Parasponia</em>-specific gain of function adaptations in HB1 may have comprised an essential evolutionary step toward functional nitrogen-fixing root nodules with rhizobium endosymbionts.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Parasponia-specific adaptations in class 1 hemoglobin protein HB1. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-20" class="first-child"><em>Parasponia</em>-specific adaptations in class 1 hemoglobin protein HB1. (<em>A</em>) Phylogenetic reconstruction of class 1 (OG0010523) and class 2 hemoglobins (OG0002188). Symbiotic hemoglobins are marked with an asterisk; legumes and the actinorhizal plant casuarina have recruited class 2 hemoglobins for balancing oxygen levels in their nodules. Conversely, <em>Parasponia</em> has recruited a class 1 hemoglobin <em>PanHB1</em>, confirming parallel evolution of symbiotic oxygen transport in this lineage: <em>M. truncatula</em> (Medtr), <em>G. max</em> (Glyma), <em>P. trichocarpa</em> (Potri), <em>F. vesca</em> (Fvesca), <em>E. grandis</em> (Eugr), <em>A. thaliana</em> (AT). (<em>D</em>) Protein sequence alignment of class 1 hemoglobins from <em>Parasponia</em> spp.,<em>Trema</em> spp., hop (<em>H. lupulus</em>), and mulberry (<em>M. notabilis</em>). Only amino acids that differ from the consensus are drawn. A linear model of the crystal structure showing α-helices and turns is depicted above the consensus sequence. There are seven amino acids (marked gray) that consistently differ between all <em>Parasponia</em> and all other sampled species: Ala(21), Gln(35), Asp(97), Ile(101), Thr(108), Val(144), and Phe(155). These differences therefore correlate with the functional divergence between <em>P. andersonii</em> PanHB1 and <em>T. tomentosa</em> TtoHB1 (<a id="xref-ref-47-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Parallel Loss of Symbiosis Genes in <em>Trema</em> and Other Relatives of <em>Parasponia</em>.</h3><p id="p-21">Evolution of complex genetic traits is often associated with gene copy number variations (CNVs) (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). To test if CNVs were associated with the generally assumed independent evolution of nodulation in <em>Parasponia</em>, we focused on two gene sets: (<em>i</em>) close homologs and putative orthologs of the genes that were characterized to function in legume-rhizobium symbiosis and (<em>ii</em>) genes with a nodule-enhanced expression and functional annotation in <em>P. andersonii</em> (these sets partially overlap and together comprise 1,813 genes; <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S14</a>). We discarded <em>Trema</em>-specific duplications as we considered them irrelevant for the nodulation phenotype. To ensure that our findings are consistent between the <em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> genera and not the result of species-specific events, we analyzed the additional draft genome assemblies of two <em>Parasponia</em> and two <em>Trema</em> species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>). As these additional draft genomes were relatively fragmented, we sought additional support for presence and absence of genes by mapping sequence reads to the <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> reference genomes and by genomic alignments. This procedure revealed only 11 consistent CNVs in the 1,813 symbiosis genes examined, further supporting the recent divergence between <em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S15</a>). Because of the dominant inheritance of nodule organogenesis in F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants, we anticipated finding <em>Parasponia</em>-specific gene duplications that could be uniquely associated with nodulation. Surprisingly, we found only one consistent <em>Parasponia</em>-specific duplication in symbiosis genes, namely, for a <em>HYDROXYCINNAMOYL-COA SHIKIMATE TRANSFERASE</em> (<em>HCT</em>; <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S16 and S17</a>). This gene has been investigated in the legume forage crop alfalfa (<em>Medicago sativa</em>), in which it was shown that <em>HCT</em> expression correlates negatively with nodule organogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>, <a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Therefore, we do not consider this duplication relevant for the nodulation capacity of <em>Parasponia</em>. Additionally, we identified three consistent gene losses in <em>Parasponia</em>, among which is the ortholog of <em>EXOPOLYSACCHARIDE RECEPTOR 3</em> that, in lotus, inhibits infection of rhizobia with incompatible exopolysaccharides (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>, <a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S18–S20 and Table S7</a>). Such gene losses may have contributed to effective rhizobium infection in <em>Parasponia</em>, and their presence in <em>T. tomentosa</em> could explain the lack of intracellular infection in the F<sub>1</sub> hybrid nodules. However, they cannot explain the dominance of nodule organogenesis in the F<sub>1</sub> hybrid.</p><p id="p-22">Contrary to our initial expectations, we discovered consistent loss or pseudogenization of seven symbiosis genes in <em>Trema</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S21–S23 and Table S7</a>). Based on our current sampling, these genes have a nodule-specific expression profile in <em>P. andersonii</em>, suggesting that they function exclusively in symbiosis (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Three of these are orthologs of genes that are essential for establishment of nitrogen-fixing nodules in legumes: <em>NIN</em>, <em>RPG</em>, and the LysM-type LCO receptor <em>NFP</em>/<em>NFR5</em>. In the case of <em>NFP/NFR5</em>, we found two close homologs of this gene, <em>NFP1</em> and <em>NFP2</em>, a duplication that predates the divergence of legumes and <em>Parasponia</em> (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). In contrast to <em>NFP1</em>, <em>NFP2</em> is consistently pseudogenized in <em>Trema</em> species (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S22 and S23</a>). In an earlier study, we used RNAi to target <em>PanNFP1</em> (previously named <em>PaNFP</em>), which led to reduced nodule numbers and a block of intracellular infection by rhizobia as well as arbuscular mycorrhiza (<a id="xref-ref-30-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). However, we cannot rule out that the RNAi construct unintentionally also targeted <em>PanNFP2</em>, as both genes are ∼70% identical in the 422-bp RNAi target region. Therefore, the precise functioning of both receptors in rhizobium and mycorrhizal symbiosis remains to be elucidated. Based on phylogenetic analysis, the newly discovered <em>PanNFP2</em> is the ortholog of the legume <em>MtNFP/LjNFR5</em> genes encoding rhizobium LCO receptors required for nodulation, whereas <em>PanNFP1</em> is most likely a paralog (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). Also, <em>PanNFP2</em> is significantly more highly expressed in nodules than <em>PanNFP1</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S25</a>). Taken together, this indicates that PanNFP2 may represent a key LCO receptor required for nodulation in <em>Parasponia</em>.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Expression profile of P. andersonii symbiosis genes that are lost in Trema species.
