Included species are <em>P. andersonii</em> (Pan), <em>Parasponia rigida</em> (Pri), <em>Parasponia rugosa</em> (Pru), <em>T. orientalis</em> RG33 (Tor), <em>T. orientalis</em> RG16 (TorRG16), <em>T. levigata</em> (Tle), medicago (<em>M. truncatula</em>, Mt), lotus (<em>L. japonicus</em>, Lj), soybean (<em>G. max</em>, Glyma), peach (<em>P. persica</em>, Ppe), woodland strawberry (<em>F. vesca</em>, Fvesca), black cotton poplar (<em>P. trichocarpa</em>, Potri), eucalyptus (<em>E. grandis</em>, Eugr), jujube (<em>Z. jujuba</em>), apple (<em>M.</em> × <em>domestica</em>), mulberry (<em>M. notabilis</em>), hops (<em>H. lupulus</em>), cassava (<em>Manihot esculenta</em>), rice (<em>O. sativa</em>), tomato (<em>S. lycopersicum</em>), and castor bean (<em>Ricinus communis</em>). Node numbers indicate posterior probabilities below 1; scale bar represents substitutions per site. <em>Parasponia</em> proteins are marked in blue, <em>Trema</em> in red.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">Based on expression profiles and phylogenetic relationships, we also postulate that <em>Parasponia NIN</em> and <em>RPG</em> commit essential symbiotic functions similarly as in other nodulating species (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S25–S28</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>, <a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>, <a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). Compared with uninoculated roots, expression of <em>PanRPG</em> is &gt;300-fold higher in <em>P. andersonii</em> nodules that become intracellularly infected (nodule stage 2), whereas, in F<sub>1</sub> hybrid nodules, which are devoid of intracellular rhizobium infection, this difference is less than 20-fold (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). This suggests that PanRPG commits a function in rhizobium infection, similarly as found in medicago (<a id="xref-ref-37-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>). The transcription factor NIN has been studied in several legume species as well as in the actinorhizal plant casuarina (<em>Casuarina glauca</em>) and, in all cases, shown to be essential for nodule organogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-17-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-54-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>, <a id="xref-ref-55-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). Loss of <em>NIN</em> and possibly <em>NFP2</em> in <em>Trema</em> species can explain the genetic dominance of nodule organogenesis in the <em>Parasponia</em> × <em>Trema</em> F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants.</p><p id="p-26">Next, we assessed whether loss of these symbiosis genes also occurred in more distant relatives of <em>Parasponia</em>. We analyzed nonnodulating species representing six additional lineages of the Rosales clade, namely hop (<em>Humulus lupulus</em>, Cannabaceae) (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>), mulberry (<em>Morus notabilis</em>, Moraceae) (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>), jujube (<em>Ziziphus jujuba</em>, Rhamnaceae) (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>), peach (<em>Prunus persica</em>, Rosaceae) (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>), woodland strawberry (<em>Fragaria vesca</em>, Rosaceae) (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>), and apple (<em>Malus × domestica</em>, Rosaceae) (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>). This revealed a consistent pattern of pseudogenization or loss of <em>NFP2</em>, <em>NIN</em>, and <em>RPG</em> orthologs, the intact jujube <em>ZjNIN</em> being the only exception (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). We note that, for peach, <em>NIN</em> was previously annotated as a protein-coding gene (<a id="xref-ref-59-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). However, based on comparative analysis of conserved exon structures, we found two out-of-frame mutations (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S28</a>). We therefore conclude that the <em>NIN</em> gene is also pseudogenized in peach. Because the pseudogenized symbiosis genes are largely intact in most of these species and differ in their deleterious mutations, the loss of function of these essential symbiosis genes should have occurred relatively recently and in parallel in at least seven Rosales lineages.</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Parallel loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating Rosales species. Pseudogenization or loss of NFP2, NIN, and RPG in two phylogenetically independent Trema lineages, H. lupulus (hop), M. notabilis (mulberry), Z. jujuba (jujube), P. persica (peach), and M. &#xD7; domestica (apple). In H. lupulus, NIN is pseudogenized, whereas NFP2 and RPG were not found (this may be because of the low N50 of the publicly available assembly). In Z. jujuba, NFP2 is lost and RPG is pseudogenized, but NIN is intact. In F. vesca, all three genes are lost (not shown). Introns are indicated but not scaled. Triangles indicate frame shifts; &#x201C;X&#x201D; indicates premature stop codons; &#x201C;LTR&#x201D; indicates LTR retrotransposon insertion (not scaled); arrows indicate alternative transcriptional start site in NIN. CD, 4 conserved domains (gray); LysM, 3 Lysin motif domains (magenta); NT-C2, N-terminal C2 domain (green); PB1, Phox and Bem1 domain (yellow); PK, protein kinase (pink); RWP-RK, conserved amino acid domain (orange); SP, signal peptide (red); TM, transmembrane domain (lilac)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-509140882" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Parallel loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating Rosales species. Pseudogenization or loss of &lt;em&gt;NFP2&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;NIN&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;RPG&lt;/em&gt; in two phylogenetically independent &lt;em&gt;Trema&lt;/em&gt; lineages, &lt;em&gt;H. lupulus&lt;/em&gt; (hop), &lt;em&gt;M. notabilis&lt;/em&gt; (mulberry), &lt;em&gt;Z. jujuba&lt;/em&gt; (jujube), &lt;em&gt;P. persica&lt;/em&gt; (peach), and &lt;em&gt;M. &#xD7; domestica&lt;/em&gt; (apple). In &lt;em&gt;H. lupulus&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;NIN&lt;/em&gt; is pseudogenized, whereas &lt;em&gt;NFP2&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;RPG&lt;/em&gt; were not found (this may be because of the low N50 of the publicly available assembly). In &lt;em&gt;Z. jujuba&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;NFP2&lt;/em&gt; is lost and &lt;em&gt;RPG&lt;/em&gt; is pseudogenized, but &lt;em&gt;NIN&lt;/em&gt; is intact. In &lt;em&gt;F. vesca&lt;/em&gt;, all three genes are lost (not shown). Introns are indicated but not scaled. Triangles indicate frame shifts; &#x201C;X&#x201D; indicates premature stop codons; &#x201C;LTR&#x201D; indicates LTR retrotransposon insertion (not scaled); arrows indicate alternative transcriptional start site in &lt;em&gt;NIN&lt;/em&gt;. CD, 4 conserved domains (gray); LysM, 3 Lysin motif domains (magenta); NT-C2, N-terminal C2 domain (green); PB1, Phox and Bem1 domain (yellow); PK, protein kinase (pink); RWP-RK, conserved amino acid domain (orange); SP, signal peptide (red); TM, transmembrane domain (lilac).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="147"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="147"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/115/20/E4700/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/809667" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-27" class="first-child">Parallel loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating Rosales species. Pseudogenization or loss of <em>NFP2</em>, <em>NIN</em>, and <em>RPG</em> in two phylogenetically independent <em>Trema</em> lineages, <em>H. lupulus</em> (hop), <em>M. notabilis</em> (mulberry), <em>Z. jujuba</em> (jujube), <em>P. persica</em> (peach), and <em>M. × domestica</em> (apple). In <em>H. lupulus</em>, <em>NIN</em> is pseudogenized, whereas <em>NFP2</em> and <em>RPG</em> were not found (this may be because of the low N50 of the publicly available assembly). In <em>Z. jujuba</em>, <em>NFP2</em> is lost and <em>RPG</em> is pseudogenized, but <em>NIN</em> is intact. In <em>F. vesca</em>, all three genes are lost (not shown). Introns are indicated but not scaled. Triangles indicate frame shifts; “X” indicates premature stop codons; “LTR” indicates LTR retrotransposon insertion (not scaled); arrows indicate alternative transcriptional start site in <em>NIN</em>. CD, 4 conserved domains (gray); LysM, 3 Lysin motif domains (magenta); NT-C2, N-terminal C2 domain (green); PB1, Phox and Bem1 domain (yellow); PK, protein kinase (pink); RWP-RK, conserved amino acid domain (orange); SP, signal peptide (red); TM, transmembrane domain (lilac).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-28" class="flushleft">Here we present the nodulating nonlegume <em>Parasponia</em> as a comparative system to obtain insights in molecular genetic changes underlying evolution of nitrogen-fixing root nodules. We show that nodulation is a genetically dominant trait and that <em>P. andersonii</em> and the legume medicago share a set of 290 genes that have a nodule-enhanced expression profile. Among these are <em>NIN</em> and <em>RPG</em>, two genes that, in legumes, are essential for nitrogen-fixing root nodulation (<a id="xref-ref-17-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-37-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>, <a id="xref-ref-54-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). Both of these genes, as well as a putative ortholog of the NFP/NFR5-type LysM receptor for rhizobium LCO signal molecules—named <em>NFP2</em> in <em>Parasponia</em>—are consistently pseudogenized or lost in <em>Trema</em> and other nonnodulating species of the Rosales order. This challenges the current view on the evolution of nitrogen-fixing plant–microbe symbioses.</p><p id="p-29">Evolution of nodulation is generally viewed as a two-step process: first an unspecified predisposition event in the ancestor of all nodulating species, bringing species in the nitrogen-fixing clade to a precursor state for nodulation; and subsequently, nodulation originated in parallel: eight times with <em>Frankia</em> and twice with rhizobium (<a id="xref-ref-1-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a><a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-7"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-9"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-10"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-11"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). This hypothesis is most parsimonious and suggests a minimum number of independent gains and losses of symbiosis. Based on this hypothesis, it is currently assumed that nonhost relatives of nodulating species are generally in a precursor state for nodulation (<a id="xref-ref-9-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>).</p><p id="p-30">Our results are difficult to explain under the hypothesis of parallel origins of nodulation. The functions of <em>NFP2</em>, <em>NIN</em>, and <em>RPG</em> currently cannot be linked to any nonsymbiotic processes. Therefore, it remains obscure why these symbiosis genes were maintained over an extended period of time in nonnodulating plant species and were subsequently independently lost. Additionally, the hypothesis of parallel origins of nodulation would imply convergent recruitment of at least 290 genes to commit symbiotic functions in <em>Parasponia</em> and legumes. Because these 290 genes encode proteins with various predicted functions (e.g., from extracellular signaling receptors to sugar transporters; <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>), as well as comprise at least two different developmental expression patterns (nodule organogenesis and intracellular infection and/or fixation; <a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>), this would imply parallel evolution of a genetically complex trait.</p><p id="p-31">Alternatively, the parallel loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating plants can be interpreted as parallel loss of nodulation (<a id="xref-ref-1-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Under this hypothesis, nodulation possibly evolved only once in an ancestor of the nitrogen-fixing clade. Subsequently, nodulation was lost in most descendant lineages. This single-gain/massive-loss hypothesis fits our data better in two ways. First, a single gain explains the origin of the conserved set of at least 290 symbiosis genes utilized by <em>Parasponia</em> and medicago because they then result from the same ancestral recruitment event. Second, it more convincingly explains the parallel loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating plants because then gene loss correlates directly with loss of nodulation. Additionally, the single-gain/massive-loss model eliminates the predisposition event, a theoretical concept that currently cannot be addressed experimentally. We therefore favor this alternative hypothesis over the currently most widely held assumption of parallel origins of nodulation.</p><p id="p-32">Loss of nodulation is not controversial, as it is generally considered to have occurred at least 20 times in the legume family (<a id="xref-ref-9-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>, <a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Nevertheless, the single-gain/massive-loss hypothesis implies many more evolutionary events than the current hypothesis of parallel gains. On the contrary, it is conceptually easier to lose a complex trait, such as nodulation, than to gain it (<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Genetic studies in legumes demonstrated that nitrogen-fixing symbioses can be abolished by a single KO mutation in tens of different genes, among which are <em>NFP/NFR5</em>, <em>NIN</em>, and <em>RPG</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). Because parsimony implies equal weights for gains and losses, it therefore may not be the best way to model the evolution of nodulation.</p><p id="p-33">Preliminary support for the single-gain/massive-loss hypothesis can be found in fossil records. Putative root nodule fossils have been discovered from the late Cretaceous (∼84 Mya), which corroborates our hypothesis that nodulation is much older than is generally assumed (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>). Legumes are the oldest and most diverse nodulating lineage, but the earliest fossils that can be definitively assigned to the legume family appeared in the late Paleocene (∼65 Mya) (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>). Notably, the age of the nodule fossils coincides with the early diversification of the nitrogen-fixing clade that has given rise to the four orders Fabales, Rosales, Cucurbitales, and Fagales (<a id="xref-ref-10-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). As it is generally agreed that individual fossil ages provide minimum bounds for dates of origins, it is therefore not unlikely that the last common ancestor of the nitrogen-fixing clade was a nodulator.</p><p id="p-34">Clearly, the single-gain/massive-loss hypothesis that is supported by our comparative studies with <em>Parasponia</em> requires further substantiation. First, the hypothesis implies that many ancestral species in the nitrogen-fixing clade were able to nodulate. This should be further supported by fossil evidence. Second, the hypothesis implies that actinorhizal plant species maintained <em>NIN</em>, <em>RPG</em>, and possibly <em>NFP2</em> (the latter only in case LCOs are used as symbiotic signal) (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>). Third, these genes should be essential for nodulation in these actinorhizal plants as well as in <em>Parasponia</em>. This can be shown experimentally, as was done for <em>NIN</em> in casuarina (<a id="xref-ref-55-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>).</p><p id="p-35">Loss of symbiosis genes in nonnodulating plant species is not absolute, as we observed a functional copy of <em>NIN</em> in jujube. This pattern is similar to the pattern of gene loss in species that lost endomycorrhizal symbiosis in which, occasionally, endomycorrhizal symbiosis genes have been maintained in nonmycorrhizal plants (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>, <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>). Conservation of <em>NIN</em> in jujube suggests that this gene has a nonsymbiotic function. Contrary to <em>NFP2</em>, which is the result of a gene duplication near the origin of the nitrogen-fixing clade, functional copies of <em>NIN</em> are also present in species outside the nitrogen-fixing clade (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S26</a>). This suggests that these genes may have retained—at least in part—an unknown ancestral nonsymbiotic function in some lineages within the nitrogen-fixing clade. Alternatively, <em>NIN</em> may have acquired a new nonsymbiotic function within some lineages in the nitrogen-fixing clade.</p><p id="p-36">As hemoglobin is crucial for rhizobium symbiosis in legumes (<a id="xref-ref-3-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>), it is striking that <em>Parasponia</em> and legumes do not use orthologous copies of hemoglobin genes in their nodules (<a id="xref-ref-47-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). Superficially, this seems inconsistent with a single gain of nodulation. However, hemoglobin is not crucial for all nitrogen-fixing nodule symbioses because several <em>Frankia</em> microsymbionts possess intrinsic physical characteristics to protect the Nitrogenase enzyme for oxidation (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">67</a><a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-68"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-69"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>). In line with this, <em>Ceanothus</em> spp. (Rhamnaceae, Rosales)—which represent actinorhizal nodulating relatives of <em>Parasponia</em>—do not express a hemoglobin gene in their <em>Frankia-</em>infected nodules (<a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a><a id="xref-ref-69-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-69"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>). Consequently, hemoglobins may have been recruited in parallel after the initial gain of nodulation as parallel adaptations to rhizobium microsymbionts. Based on the fact that <em>Parasponia</em> acquired lineage-specific adaptations in HB1 that are considered to be essential for controlling oxygen homeostasis in rhizobium root nodules (<a id="xref-ref-47-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>), a symbiont switch from <em>Frankia</em> to rhizobium may have occurred recently in an ancestor of the <em>Parasponia</em> lineage.</p><p id="p-37">Our study provides leads for attempts to engineer nitrogen-fixing root nodules in agricultural crop plants. Such a translational approach is anticipated to be challenging (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">71</a>), and the only published attempt so far, describing transfer of eight LCO signaling genes, was unsuccessful (<a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">72</a>). Our results suggest that transfer of symbiosis genes may not be sufficient to obtain functional nodules. Even though F<sub>1</sub> hybrid plants contain a full haploid genome complement of <em>P. andersonii</em>, they lack intracellular infection. This may be the result of haploinsufficiency of <em>P. andersoni</em>i genes in the F<sub>1</sub> hybrid or because of an inhibitory factor in <em>T. tomentosa</em>. For example, inhibition of intracellular infection may be the result of a dominant-negative factor or the result of heterozygosity negatively affecting the formation of, e.g., LysM receptor complexes required for appropriate perception of microsymbionts. Such factors may also be present in other nonhost species. Consequently, engineering nitrogen-fixing nodules may require gene KOs in nonnodulating plants to overcome inhibition of intracellular infection. <em>Trema</em> may be the best candidate species for such a (re)engineering approach because of its high genetic similarity with <em>Parasponi</em>a and the availability of transformation protocols (<a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">73</a>). Therefore, the <em>Parasponia</em>–<em>Trema</em> comparative system may not only be suited for evolutionary studies, but also can form an experimental platform to obtain essential insights for engineering nitrogen-fixing root nodules.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3><em>Parasponia</em>–<em>Trema</em> Intergeneric Crossing and Hybrid Genotyping.</h3><p id="p-38"><em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> are wind-pollinated species. A female-flowering <em>P. andersonii</em> individual WU1.14 was placed in a plastic shed together with a flowering <em>T. tomentosa</em> WU10 plant. Putative F<sub>1</sub> hybrid seeds were germinated (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>) and transferred to potting soil. To confirm the hybrid genotype, a PCR marker was used that visualizes a length difference in the promoter region of <em>LIKE-AUXIN 1</em> (<em>LAX1</em>; primers, LAX1-forward, ACATGATAATTTGGGCATGCAACA; LAX1-reverse, TCCCGAATTTTCTACGAATTGAAA; amplicon size, <em>P. andersonii</em>, 974 bp; <em>T. tomentosa</em>, 483 bp). Hybrid plant H9 was propagated in vitro (<a id="xref-ref-30-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>, <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>). The karyotype of the selected plants was determined according to Geurts and de Jong (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">75</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Assembly of Reference Genomes.</h3><p id="p-39">Cleaned DNA sequencing reads were de novo assembled by using ALLPATHS-LG (release 48961) (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">76</a>). After filtering of any remaining adapters and contamination, contigs were scaffolded with two rounds of SSPACE-standard (v3.0) (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>) with the mate-pair libraries using default settings. We used the output of the second run of SSPACE scaffolding as the final assembly (full details and parameter choices are provided in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). Validation of the final assemblies showed that 90–100% of the genomic reads mapped back to the assemblies (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a>), and 94–98% of CEGMA (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>) and BUSCO (<a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">79</a>) genes were detected (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Annotation of Reference Genomes.</h3><p id="p-40">Repetitive elements were identified following the standard Maker-P recipe (<a href="http://weatherby.genetics.utah.edu/MAKER/wiki/index.php/Repeat_Library_Construction-Advanced" class="in-nw">weatherby.genetics.utah.edu/MAKER/wiki/index.php/Repeat_Library_Construction-Advanced</a>, accessed October 2015) as described on the GMOD site: (<em>i</em>) RepeatModeler with Repeatscout v1.0.5, Recon v1.08, RepeatMasker version open4.0.5, using RepBase version 20140131 (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">80</a>) and TandemRepeatFinder; (<em>ii</em>) GenomeTools LTRharvest and LTRdigest (<a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a>); (<em>iii</em>) MITEhunter with default parameters (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">82</a>). We generated species-specific repeat libraries for <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> separately and combined these into a single repeat library, filtering out sequences that are &gt;98% similar. We masked both genomes by using RepeatMasker with this shared repeat library.</p><p id="p-41">To aid the structural annotation, we used 11 <em>P. andersonii</em> and 6 <em>T. orientalis</em> RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) datasets (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S8</a>). All RNA-seq samples were assembled de novo by using genome-guided Trinity (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">83</a>), resulting in one combined transcriptome assembly per species. In addition, all samples were mapped to their respective reference genomes by using BWA-MEM and processed into putative transcripts by using cufflinks (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">84</a>) and transdecoder (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">85</a>). As protein homology evidence, only UniProt (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">86</a>) entries filtered for plant proteins were used. This way we included only manually verified protein sequences and prevented the incorporation of erroneous predictions. Finally, four gene-predictor tracks were used: (<em>i</em>) SNAP (<a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">87</a>) trained on <em>P. andersonii</em> transdecoder transcript annotations; (<em>ii</em>) SNAP trained on <em>T. orientalis</em> transdecoder transcript annotations; (<em>iii</em>) Augustus (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>), as used in the BRAKER pipeline, trained on RNA-seq alignments (<a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">89</a>); and (<em>iv</em>) GeneMark-ET, as used in the BRAKER pipeline, trained on RNA-seq alignments (<a id="xref-ref-90-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">90</a>).</p><p id="p-42">First, all evidence tracks were processed by Maker-P (<a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">91</a>). The results were refined with EVidenceModeler (EVM) (<a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">92</a>), which was used with all of the same tracks as Maker-P, except for the Maker-P blast tracks and with the addition of the Maker-P consensus track as additional evidence. Ultimately, EVM gene models were preferred over Maker-P gene models except when there was no overlapping EVM gene model. Where possible, evidence of both species was used to annotate each genome (i.e., de novo RNA-seq assemblies of both species were aligned to both genomes).</p><p id="p-43">To take maximum advantage of annotating two highly similar genomes simultaneously, we developed a custom reconciliation procedure involving whole-genome alignments. The consensus annotations from merging the EVM and Maker-P annotations were transferred to their respective partner genome by using nucmer (<a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">93</a>) and RATT revision 18 (<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">94</a>) (i.e., the <em>P. andersonii</em> annotation was transferred to <em>T. orientalis</em> and vice versa) based on nucmer whole-genome alignments (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S10</a>). Through this reciprocal transfer, both genomes had two candidate annotation tracks. This allowed for validation of annotation differences between <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em>, reduced technical variation, and consequently improved all downstream analyses. After automatic annotation and reconciliation, 1,693 <em>P. andersonii</em> genes and 1,788 <em>T. orientalis</em> genes were manually curated. These were mainly homologs of legume symbiosis genes and genes that were selected based on initial data exploration.</p><p id="p-44">To assign putative product names to the predicted genes, we combined BLAST results against UniProt, TrEMBL, and nr with InterProScan results (custom script). To annotate Gene Ontology (GO) terms and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) enzyme codes we used Blast2GO based on the nr BLAST results and InterProScan results. Finally, we filtered all gene models with hits to InterPro domains that are specific to repetitive elements.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Orthogroup Inference.</h3><p id="p-45">To determine relationships between <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> genes, as well as with other plant species, we inferred orthogroups with OrthoFinder version 0.4.0 (<a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">95</a>). As orthogroups are defined as the set of genes that are descended from a single gene in the last common ancestor of all of the species being considered, they can comprise orthologous as well as paralogous genes. Our analysis included proteomes of selected species from the Eurosid clade: <em>A</em>. <em>thaliana</em> TAIR10 (Brassicaceae, Brassicales) (<a id="xref-ref-96-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">96</a>) and <em>Eucalyptus grandis</em> v2.0 (Myrtaceae, Myrtales) from the Malvid clade (<a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">97</a>); <em>Populus trichocarpa</em> v3.0 (Salicaeae, Malpighiales) (<a id="xref-ref-98-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">98</a>), legumes <em>M. truncatula</em> Mt4.0v1 (<a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">99</a>) and <em>G</em>. <em>max</em> Wm82.a2.v1 (Fabaceae, Fabales) (<a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">100</a>), <em>F. vesca</em> v1.1 (Rosaceae, Rosales) (<a id="xref-ref-60-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>), and <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. orientalis</em> (Cannabaceae, Rosales) from the Fabid clade (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). Sequences were retrieved from phytozome (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/" class="in-nw">www.phytozome.net</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Gene CNV Detection.</h3><p id="p-46">To assess orthologous and paralogous relationships between <em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> genes, we inferred phylogenetic gene trees for all 21,959 orthogroups comprising <em>Parasponia</em> and/or <em>Trema</em> genes by using the neighbor-joining clustering algorithm (<a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">101</a>). Based on these gene trees, for each <em>Parasponia</em> gene, its relationship to other <em>Parasponia</em> and <em>Trema</em> genes was defined as follows: (<em>i</em>) orthologous pair indicates that the sister lineage is a single gene from the <em>Trema</em> genome, suggesting that they are the result of a speciation event; (<em>ii</em>) inparalog indicates that the sister lineage is a gene from the <em>Parasponia</em> genome, suggesting that they are the result of a gene duplication event; (<em>iii</em>) singleton indicates that the sister lineage is a gene from a species other than <em>Trema</em>, suggesting that the <em>Trema</em> gene was lost; and (<em>iv</em>) multiortholog indicates that the sister lineage comprises multiple genes from the <em>Trema</em> genome, suggesting that the latter are inparalogs. For each <em>Trema</em> gene, the relationship was defined in the same way but with respect to the <em>Parasponia</em> genome (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>). Because phylogenetic analysis relies on homology, we assessed the level of conservation in the multiple-sequence alignments by calculating the trident score using MstatX (<a href="https://github.com/gcollet/MstatX" class="in-nw">https://github.com/gcollet/MstatX</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-102-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">102</a>). Orthogroups with a score below 0.1 were excluded from the analysis. Examination of orthogroups comprising &gt;20 inparalogs revealed that some represented repetitive elements; these were also excluded. Finally, orthologous pairs were validated based on the whole-genome alignments used in the annotation reconciliation.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Nodule-Enhanced Genes.</h3><p id="p-47">To assess gene expression in <em>Parasponia</em> nodules, RNA was sequenced from the three nodule stages described earlier as well as uninoculated roots (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S8</a>). RNA-seq reads were mapped to the <em>Parasponia</em> reference genome with HISAT2 version 2.02 (<a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">103</a>) using an index that includes exon and splice site information in the RNA-seq alignments. Mapped reads were assigned to transcripts with featureCounts version 1.5.0 (<a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">104</a>). Normalization and differential gene expression were performed with DESeq2. Nodule enhanced genes were selected based on &gt;2.0-fold change and <em>P</em> ≤ 0.05 in any nodule stage compared with uninoculated root controls. Genes without functional annotation or orthogroup membership or from orthogroups with low alignment scores (&lt;0.1 trident score, as detailed earlier) or representing repetitive elements were excluded from further analysis. To assess expression of <em>Parasponia</em> genes in the hybrid nodules, RNA was sequenced from nodules and uninoculated roots. Here, RNA-seq reads were mapped to a combined reference comprising two parent genomes from <em>P. andersonii</em> and <em>T. tomentosa</em>. To assess which genes are nodule-enhanced in medicago, we reanalyzed published RNA-seq read data from Roux et al. (<a id="xref-ref-34-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) [archived at the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) under sequence read archive (SRA) study ID code SRP028599]. To assess which of these genes may be coopted from the ancient and widespread arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis, we generated a set of 575 medicago genes induced upon mycorrhization in medicago by reanalyzing published RNA-seq read data from Afkhami and Stinchcombe (archived at the NCBI under SRA study ID code SRP078249) (<a id="xref-ref-46-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>) Both medicago data sets were analyzed as described earlier for <em>Parasponia</em> but by using the medicago genome and annotation version 4.0v2 as reference (<a id="xref-ref-99-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">99</a>).</p><p id="p-48">To assess common recruitment of genes in nodules from <em>Parasponia</em> and medicago, we counted orthogroups comprising <em>P. andersonii</em> and medicago nodule-enhanced genes. To assess whether this number is higher than expected by chance, we performed the hypergeometric test as well as three different permutation tests in which we randomized the <em>Parasponia</em> gene set, the medicago gene set, or both sets with 10,000 permutations. We then determined putative orthology between the <em>Parasponia</em> and medicago genes within the common orthogroups based on phylogenetic analysis. <em>Parasponia</em> and medicago genes were considered putative orthogroups if they occurred in the same subclade with more than 50% bootstrap support; otherwise, they were considered close homologs.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Availability of Data and Materials.</h3><p id="p-49">The data reported in this study are tabulated in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S1</a>–<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">S7</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1721395115/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>; sequence data are archived at NCBI (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/" class="in-nw">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) under BioProject numbers PRJNA272473 and PRJNA272482; draft genome assemblies, phylogenetic datasets, and orthogroup data are archived at the Dryad Digital Repository (<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.fq7gv88" class="in-nw">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.fq7gv88</a>). Analyzed data can also be browsed or downloaded through a Web portal at <a href="http://www.parasponia.org/" class="in-nw">www.parasponia.org</a>. All custom scripts and code are available online at <a href="https://github.com/holmrenser/parasponia_code" class="in-nw">https://github.com/holmrenser/parasponia_code</a>.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-50">We thank Shelley James, Thomas Marler, Giles Oldroyd, and Johan van Valkenburg for providing germplasm and Ries deVisser (IsoLife) for supporting acetylene reduction assays.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-3"><p id="p-3"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-3-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>R.v.V. and R. class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a href="/lookup/ijlink/YTozOntzOjQ6InBhdGgiO3M6MTQ6Ii9sb29rdXAvaWpsaW5rIjtzOjU6InF1ZXJ5IjthOjQ6e3M6ODoibGlua1R5cGUiO3M6NDoiQUJTVCI7czoxMToiam91cm5hbENvZGUiO3M6Mzoic2NpIjtzOjU6InJlc2lkIjtzOjEzOiIzNTMvNjMwNS8xMjI1IjtzOjQ6ImF0b20iO3M6MjM6Ii9wbmFzLzExNS8yMC9FNDcwMC5hdG9tIjt9czo4OiJmcmFnbWVudCI7czowOiIiO30=" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-ijlink"><span><span class="cit-reflinks-abstract">Abstract</span><span class="cit-sep cit-reflinks-variant-name-sep">/</span><span
